    [Original] IF Copack LLC dba Initiative Foods Recalls â€œTippy Toesâ€ Brand Apple Pear Banana Because of Possible Health Risk
    More Recalls, Market Withdrawals, & Safety Alerts Sanger, CA â€“ February 13, 2026 â€“ IF Copack LLC d.b.a. Initiative Foods (â€œInitiative Foodsâ€) is recalling one lot of the â€œTippy Toesâ€ brand Apple Pear Banana Fruit puree (â€œProductâ€) due to elevated levels of patulin. Patulin is a naturally occurring substance (called a mycotoxin) which is produced by molds that may grow in various fruits, including apples. Long-term exposure resulting from ingestion of patulin can lead to various adverse health consequences, including a potential for immune suppression, nerve damage, headache, fever, and nausea. No illnesses or injuries have been reported to date. The Product was distributed nationwide in retail grocery stores in all U.S. states other than Alaska. The Product may have been distributed in the U.S. territories of Guam and Puerto Rico. 2-pack plastic tubs, with product information sleeve The â€œBest Byâ€ (expiration) date is found on the bottom of each plastic tub. The recalled Product will have a date stamped as â€œBB 07/17/2026.â€ The package is also marked with the package code â€œINIA0120â€ as shown below: The recalled Product was sampled under the Total Diet Study by the U.S. Food and Drug Administration (â€œFDAâ€), which found elevated patulin levels higher than is common for these products. Initiative Foods worked with FDA to identify the single lot to recall due to possible health concerns identified in this notice. This recall is being conducted based on FDAâ€™s recommendation. Consumers with the Product matching the â€œBest Byâ€ (expiration) date of July 17, 2026 should: Retailers should check inventory and shelves, and immediately remove the affected lot from sale or distribution and catalogue the recalled product. â€œAt Initiative Foods, the safety of our consumers and their families is our highest priority. We are cooperating with the FDA to ensure strict review and enhanced safety measures across all our products. We thank our retail partners and customers for their understanding and prompt action on this matter,â€ said Don Ephgrave, Initiative Foodsâ€™ CEO and President. For further recall information and updates, consumers and retailers can call a dedicated toll-free number: 1-855-215-5730, Monday through Friday, 8 a.m. to 5 p.m. Eastern Standard Time. This recall is being conducted with the full knowledge of the U.S. Food and Drug Administration.

    [Original] Ambrosia Brands, LLC Recalls Rosabella Moringa Capsules Because of Possible Health Risk
    More Recalls, Market Withdrawals, & Safety Alerts Ambrosia Brands, LLC of New York, New York is recalling certain lots of its Rosabella Moringa Capsules product due to possible contamination with Salmonella, an organism which can cause serious and sometimes fatal infections in young children, frail or elderly people, and others with weakened immune systems. Healthy persons infected with Salmonella often experience fever, diarrhea (which may be bloody), nausea, vomiting and abdominal pain. In rare circumstances, infection with Salmonella can result in the organism getting into the bloodstream and producing more severe illnesses such as arterial infections (i.e., infected aneurysms), endocarditis and arthritis. The recalled product was sold to consumers located nationwide in the United States, through our direct-to-consumer website (tryrosabella.com) and Tik Tok Shop (https://www.tiktok.com/shop/pdp/moringa-capsules-800mg-by-rosabella-for-immune-gut-health-support/1729464033034539782), since February 2025. We are also aware that there may be unauthorized 3rd party distribution to consumers through ebay.com, Shein, or other sites. None of the impacted lots were sold by us on Amazon.com, however, while we do not have any authorized resellers on Amazon.com, we urge you to check your lot numbers for any Rosabella Moringa capsules purchased on the site. The recall includes the following product and lots: The recalled products are packaged in white plastic bottles. The lot code is printed on the bottom of the bottles. The lot code is the middle seven digits of the code printed above the expiration date. Additionally, the affected product has expiration dates from 03/2027 to 11/2027. Please see the example packaging and lot code below. No other Ambrosia Brands products are impacted by this recall. To date, there have been 7 illnesses resulting in 3 hospitalizations across the United States due to Salmonella contamination, 3 of which may be linked to a single product. At this time, the FDA and CDC have reported that the outbreak may be linked to Rosabella Moringa Capsules. We continue to diligently investigate, in collaboration with FDA, this possible link of the Salmonella outbreak to Rosebella Moringa Capsules. We have discontinued use and purchase of all raw moringa leaf powder from the raw material supplier of the above referenced lots. Customers who have purchased the lots above are asked to dispose of it immediately, do not eat, sell, or serve the product. Ambrosia Brands is conducting this recall voluntarily and takes this matter very seriously. We apologize for the inconvenience and concern this recall may cause our customers. Our company is committed to ensuring the quality of our products and the well-being of our consumers. Consumers with questions may contact the company M-F from 9am-4pm CT at 914-768-1357.

    [Original] Busy with Casgevy and Journavx launches, Vertex sets ambitious $500M revenue goal for non-CF meds this year
    Vertex Pharmaceuticals is accelerating its push beyond its core cystic fibrosis (CF) franchise, aiming to generate $500 million or more in non-CF revenue in 2026, within its $12.95 billion to $13.1 billion total revenue guidance. The companyâ€™s diversification efforts center on two key products: gene therapy Casgevy, approved in 2023 for sickle cell disease and 2024 for beta thalassemia, and non-opioid pain medication Journavx, launched at the start of 2025. In 2025, Casgevy generated $116 million in sales, while Journavx brought in $59.6 million, totaling $175.6 million in non-CF revenue. Journavx, which has been well-received by patients with surgical and non-surgical pain, saw fourth-quarter sales of $26.7 millionâ€”below the $32.9 million analysts expectedâ€”but Vertex reported that approximately 420,000 Americans benefited from the drug as a well-tolerated, effective non-opioid option for moderate to severe acute pain. The company aims to triple prescriptions in 2026, up from 550,000 in 2025, with prescriptions currently split evenly between hospital and retail channels. To drive growth, Vertex is piloting a TV ad campaign in select markets and plans to double its field force this year. Casgevy showed strong momentum in Q4 2025, generating $54 million in revenueâ€”exceeding Evercore ISIâ€™s $41.1 million forecastâ€”after 11 new patients began treatment and 30 received infusions. While full-year sales of $116 million fall short of blockbuster expectations, Vertex remains confident in the therapyâ€™s â€œmulti-billion dollar potential,â€ citing steady progress across the patient journey. Executives anticipate quarterly variability in infusion rates due to the lengthy treatment process but expect smoother performance in 2027 and beyond. Meanwhile, Vertexâ€™s CF franchise remains robust. Alyftrek, a once-daily triple combination therapy launched in late 2024, generated $838 million in its first year. Recent positive results from a pediatric study in children aged 2 to 5â€”described as â€œunprecedentedâ€â€”have prompted Vertex to prepare a label expansion submission in the first half of 2026. Trikafta, Vertexâ€™s flagship CF drug, generated $10.3 billion in 2025, accounting for the majority of company revenue. Despite expected modest erosion in 2026 due to uptake of Alyftrek, William Blair analysts noted Trikaftaâ€™s â€œsurprisingly resilientâ€ performance. Together, Vertexâ€™s CF therapies now treat nearly 95% of CF patients in core markets, including those as young as one month old.

    [Original] With the FDA's Moderna decision, vaccine makers face increasingly uncertain regulatory environment
    The U.S. regulatory environment has undergone a sharp shift, exemplified by the FDAâ€™s refusal to file Modernaâ€™s application for its mRNA-based flu vaccine, mRNA-1010, despite a successful phase 3 trial involving over 40,000 adults aged 50 and older. The trial showed mRNA-1010 was 27% more effective than an FDA-approved flu shot. However, the FDA cited the use of a standard-dose vaccine in the comparator arm instead of a higher-dose, â€œbest-available standard of careâ€ shot, a design previously approved by the agency in 2024. The reversal comes amid a broader overhaul of vaccine policy under HHS Secretary Robert F. Kennedy Jr., who has appointed FDA officials skeptical of vaccines. Analyst Mani Foroohar of Leerink Partners described the shift as a â€œbait and switch,â€ noting companies developed trials based on prior regulatory feedback, now disrupted by abrupt policy changes. The decision, reportedly overruled by Vinay Prasad, head of the FDAâ€™s Center for Biologics Evaluation and Research (CBER), has introduced significant uncertainty. The Alliance for mRNA Medicines called the move â€œunprecedented,â€ warning it undermines public health and the evidence-based framework for vaccine development. Meanwhile, Moderna CEO Stephane Bancel stated the company will not pursue new phase 3 infectious disease vaccine trials due to opposition from U.S. officials, citing the risk of losing access to the U.S. market. Foroohar noted the uncertainty could deter investment, altering the risk-reward calculus for vaccine development. This regulatory volatility coincides with declining U.S. vaccine sales: Sanofi reported a 28% drop in U.S. RSV vaccine Beyfortus sales, while GSKâ€™s Shingrix sales fell 20% in the U.S. but rose 44% in Europe. Despite these challenges, Foroohar believes large pharmaceutical firms like Merck, Sanofi, Pfizer, and GSK will maintain long-term vaccine strategies, making only marginal adjustments due to their multi-decade planning horizons. Still, increasing uncertainty is likely to make companies more conservative in their investment decisions.

    [Original] Moderna R&D spend shrunk 31% in 2025 amid major pipeline reorg
    Modernaâ€™s research and development spending dropped 31% in 2025 to $3.1 billion, down from $4.5 billion in 2024 and $4.8 billion in 2023, primarily due to the wind-down of major phase 3 respiratory trials and reduced clinical development and manufacturing costs. The company cited continued portfolio prioritization and a strategic shift away from late-stage vaccine development, particularly in respiratory diseases. CEO StÃ©phane Bancel told Bloomberg TV that Moderna â€œdoes not foresee investing in new phase 3 studies in a foreseeable futureâ€ in vaccines, reflecting a broader pivot toward cancer therapies and its norovirus vaccine, currently in phase 3 with data expected later in 2026. This shift follows the federal governmentâ€™s termination of a $590 million contract supporting the development of Modernaâ€™s bird flu vaccine in May 2025, contributing to at least two rounds of layoffs and the discontinuation of four clinical mRNA programs. The company also lost Chief Medical Officer Jacqueline Miller, M.D., last month after her tenure of just over a year. The FDAâ€™s refusal-to-file letter for Modernaâ€™s next-generation flu vaccine has raised concerns across the industry, with Leerink Partners analyst Mani Foroohar, M.D., calling the decision â€œjarringâ€ given prior regulatory feedback. Financially, Moderna reported $1.9 billion in revenue for 2025, a 42% decline year over year. CFO Jamey Mack described 2025 as a â€œkey turning pointâ€ in the companyâ€™s financial story, noting that the company exceeded its cost-reduction targets by over $1 billion and ended the year with $2 billion more in cash than originally projected. For 2026, Moderna forecasts sales growth of up to 10%, with a balanced 50-50 split between U.S. and international marketsâ€”down from 62% U.S. revenue in 2025. The company plans to begin selling locally manufactured products in the U.K. and Australia in 2026, a key driver of international expansion. This guidance assumes no revenue from its investigational flu shot or its COVID/flu combination vaccine, both under review in the U.K. and Australia.

    [Original] Vertexâ€™s CRISPR therapy rebounds in latest earnings
    Sales of Casgevy, the CRISPR Therapeuticsâ€“developed gene-editing therapy for sickle cell disease and beta thalassemia, more than tripled in the fourth quarter of 2025, contributing to a 9% year-over-year revenue increase for Vertex Pharmaceuticals, which reported $12 billion in total revenueâ€”$3.2 billion in the final quarter. Casgevy generated $54 million in Q4 and $116 million for the full year, surpassing Wall Street expectations after sales of $17 million in the prior quarter. The number of patients initiating treatment nearly tripled from 109 in 2024 to 301 in 2025. The therapy involves a nearly 12-month process, beginning with stem cell collection, followed by CRISPR gene editing to enable fetal hemoglobin production, which can alleviate symptoms and potentially cure the disease. However, the chemotherapy conditioning required for infusion can cause severe side effects, including infertility, deterring some patients. Of the 64 patients whose cells were reinfused in 2025, 30 received treatment in the final quarter. TD Cowen analyst Tyler Van Buren noted sales may fluctuate quarterly due to scheduling preferences, such as timing infusions around holidays, but projected Casgevy could reach $227 million in 2026 based on ongoing patient enrollment. Vertex expects sales to â€œsmooth outâ€ by 2027, citing a â€œrobust flow of patients,â€ according to Chief Operating Officer Duncan McKechnie. The company received a priority review voucher from the FDA for Casgevy in children aged 5 to 11 and plans to seek approval in the first half of 2026. This could significantly boost future revenue. Casgevyâ€™s performance was one of two â€œpositive surprisesâ€ in the earnings report, alongside strong sales of the new cystic fibrosis drug Alyftrek. Cystic fibrosis therapies, primarily Trikafta and Kaftrio, accounted for over $10 billion of the $12 billion in 2025 revenue. Vertex forecasts $13 billion to $13.1 billion in 2026 revenue, with $500 million or more from non-CF products. Upcoming data readouts for experimental kidney drugs povetacicept and inaxaplin are key investor focus points. RBC Capital Markets estimates povetacicept, if approved for IgA nephropathy, could generate at least $1.2 billion annually.

    [Original] Amazon's Ring ends deal with surveillance firm after backlash
    Amazonâ€™s Ring has terminated its planned partnership with Flock Safety, a surveillance technology firm, following intense public backlash over privacy concerns. The deal, announced in October, would have enabled law enforcement agencies using Flockâ€™s network of cameras and license plate readers to access video from Ring doorbell devices, provided customers had consented. However, Ring stated the integration â€œnever launched,â€ meaning no customer footage was ever shared with Flock. The company cited unanticipated time and resource demands as the primary reason for the cancellation, which came just days after a Super Bowl ad for Ringâ€™s â€œSearch Partyâ€ feature sparked widespread criticism. The ad, which depicted neighbors using the tool to locate a lost dog, was condemned by critics as promoting a dystopian surveillance state. Senator Ed Markey (D-Mass.) called on Amazon to discontinue the feature, while the Electronic Frontier Foundation labeled it a cover for â€œbiometric identificationâ€ that could track people and pets. The ad drew mockery on social media and inspired a satirical response from rival Wyze, whose video parody has been viewed nearly 100,000 times. Flock Safety, founded in 2017, operates in over 5,000 U.S. cities and primarily serves police agencies. The company described the partnershipâ€™s cancellation as a â€œmutual decision,â€ stating it allows both firms to better serve their customers. The move follows growing scrutiny of both companiesâ€™ privacy practices and their ties to law enforcement. Ring, acquired by Amazon in 2018, has previously faced criticism for its data-sharing policies, especially amid heightened immigration enforcement under former President Donald Trump. Flock had also come under fire after Senator Ron Wyden (D-Ore.) accused it of insufficient safeguards, warning its systems could be used to target immigrants or women under abortion-related laws. Ring says it receives thousands of law enforcement requests annually for customer footage and complies when legally required. Despite the cancellation, both companies continue to face public skepticism over surveillance and data privacy.

    Meta, Facebookâ€™s parent company, is planning to reintroduce facial recognition technology in its smart glassesâ€”developed with EssilorLuxottica, the maker of Ray-Ban and Oakleyâ€”later this year, according to four people familiar with the plans. The feature, internally called â€œName Tag,â€ would allow users to identify people and access information via Metaâ€™s AI assistant. The company has been evaluating the rollout since early 2023, weighing safety and privacy risks, and considered a limited release to attendees of a conference for the blind before a broader launch. An internal May document cited the current political climate as favorable, noting that civil society groups may be distracted by other issues. Facial recognition has long drawn criticism for enabling surveillance by governments, corporations, and individuals, with some cities banning its use by police due to accuracy concerns. Democratic lawmakers have urged Immigration and Customs Enforcement to halt its use on public streets. Nathan Freed Wessler of the ACLU warned that such technology threatens public anonymity and is prone to abuse. Meta initially considered adding the feature to its first Ray-Ban smart glasses in 2021 but withdrew due to technical and ethical concerns. The company has since renewed efforts, driven in part by the Trump administrationâ€™s alignment with big tech and the glassesâ€™ commercial successâ€”EssilorLuxottica reported selling over seven million units last year. Meta emphasized it is still â€œthinking through optionsâ€ and will take a â€œthoughtful approachâ€ before any rollout. The company noted that users must activate the glasses to record or query the AI, though it is developing â€œsuper sensingâ€ glasses that would continuously capture data, raising further privacy concerns. Meta has a history of privacy controversies, including a $2 billion settlement in Illinois and Texas over unauthorized facial data collection and a $5 billion FTC settlement in 2019.     [Original] Iran Turns to Digital Surveillance Tools to Track Down Protesters
    When protests erupted in Iran in late December, some demonstrators received ominous text messages warning them that their presence at â€œillegal gatheringsâ€ had been recorded and that they were under â€œintelligence monitoring,â€ with authorities advising them to refrain from such activities, which were labeled as being â€œdesired by the enemy.â€ Researchers concluded the government likely tracked protesters using location data from their mobile devices, part of a broader digital surveillance campaign to identify and detain dissenters. Iran, like China, maintains one of the worldâ€™s most extensive surveillance systems, integrating mobile tracking, facial recognition, web monitoring, and a centralized National Information Network developed since 2013. This infrastructure enables authorities to filter content, block global platforms like Instagram, WhatsApp, and YouTube, and impose full internet blackouts during crises. As online access is gradually restored, security forces have detained individuals based on facial recognition and phone data, while some social media users had their SIM cards suspended or faced banking disruptions, according to Holistic Resilience, a digital rights group. The government aims to identify and arrest protest â€œleaders,â€ with one security official stating authorities can â€œfollow you to the streetsâ€ and later target individuals from a compiled list. Since 2018, Iran has expanded surveillance capabilities, including spyware that records messages and copies files, and a centralized digital identity system linking personal data to mobile usage. Programs like SIAM allow authorities to track movements, log behavior, and throttle data speeds. Domestic services, which are more easily monitored, have been promoted by blocking global platforms. Despite efforts to circumvent controlsâ€”such as using Starlinkâ€”users now risk imprisonment or execution. Fake VPN apps and counterfeit Starlink services have also been found to contain spyware. Iran has collaborated with authoritarian regimes, including Russiaâ€™s Protei and Chinese firms Huawei and ZTE, to bolster its surveillance. While not flawlessâ€”protesters reported misidentification by facial recognitionâ€”authorities have used tools like ISMI catchers and contactless ID readers in cities like Isfahan to target women who defied hijab laws, sending threatening messages based on collected data. Civil society and researchers warn of ongoing efforts to subvert digital tools meant to ensure privacy and free expression.

    [Original] In the age of AI, we must learn to act before the future is clear
    Many questions about AI, from the outcome of the AI race to speculation about an AI bubble, need more time to be answered. Uncertainty is the new normal The subject of artificial intelligence (AI) has become so prevalent in global discourse that the recent World Economic Forum in Davos reportedly had more than 200 sessions for corporate leaders to discuss AI from multiple angles. The conversations focused on five key themes: AI and technologyâ€™s role in geopolitical rivalry; AI as a productivity and growth driver; AI as a job disrupter; ethics and governance for generative AI and robot systems; and AIâ€™s environmental footprint.Underlying these themes are questions often asked by the average person. Will the US or China win the AI race? Is there an AI bubble, or is it overstated? How can companies and nations use AI to create, coordinate and capture value for both individuals and collective humanity?First, the answer to who will win the AI race is at best an opinion and not a matter of fact because the facts are not yet fully known and are changing by the day. AI relies on machine-readable data, and if the data is wrong, the conclusions are wrong. Relying on AI to interpret a real world that is far more complex and dynamic than any theory, with random factors that are seldom factored in, means that we cannot know for sure ahead of time what the future will bring, even with the best machine or expert analysis. We simply must accept that the interactively dynamic future creates multiple options with a whole range of outcomes. No one is fully in control of our future. Former US national security adviser Jake Sullivan made a shrewd observation last month in a Foreign Affairs piece he co-wrote with Tal Feldman, titled â€œGeopolitics in the Age of Artificial Intelligenceâ€. â€œWashington does not need another prediction about the AI age,â€ they wrote. â€œIt needs a way to make choices under uncertainty â€“ one that secures the United Statesâ€™ advantage across multiple possible futures and adapts as the shape of the AI era comes into view.â€ Policymakers should â€œtreat AI not as a single story but as a shifting landscapeâ€. Post Techcast: the great AI schismSecond, it is simply too early to tell whether there is an AI bubble. At Davos, Israeli historian Yuval Noah Harari likened the current moment in AI to the early Industrial Revolution, when it took generations for inventions such as the steam engine, electricity and railways to diffuse and be better understood. In situations that are new and exciting, people can sometimes hallucinate through financial speculation, such as the Railway Mania bubble in the 1840s when there was excessive investment in railway tracks and stock.

    [Original] Andrew facing claim he shared Treasury document with banking contact
    Prince Andrew Mountbatten-Windsor faces renewed scrutiny over allegations of misusing his official role, following the publication of emails revealing he shared a confidential Treasury briefing on Icelandâ€™s banking crisis with Jonathan Rowland, a personal business associate. The 2010 emails, published by the Telegraph, show Andrew requested an â€œupdate note on the latest position between the UK and Iceland on the matter of the deposits and the deposit guarantee schemeâ€ via his adviser. Shortly after receiving the document, he forwarded it to Rowland, asking for â€œcomment and a suggestion or solution,â€ while noting that UK officials were urging caution before taking action. Rowlandâ€™s father, David Rowland, had taken over the Luxembourg arm of Kaupthing, Icelandâ€™s largest bank, which later became Banque Havilland. Jonathan Rowland, who served as CEO of Banque Havilland until 2013, told the BBC he had â€œno ideaâ€ about the reference in the emails and denied any contact with Jeffrey Epstein, though he did not rule out financial ties to Andrew or his ex-wife, Sarah Ferguson, who reportedly faced debt issues and had loans from the bank. Andrew Mountbatten-Windsor has denied any wrongdoing in his associations with Epstein and has been contacted for comment. No allegations of misconduct have been made against Jonathan or David Rowland.

    [Original] Heathrow not crowded but people walk in 'wrong place', says boss
    ShareSaveMitchell LabiakBusiness reporterShareSaveGetty ImagesHeathrow Airport's Terminal 5 is not crowded â€” people are just walking in the "wrong place", the airport's boss has said. Thomas Woldbye told an industry event "all the British people keep to the left and all the Europeans keep to the right", making the terminal feel crowded "when it's not actually". "And they do that in both directions, so we get everybody crashing into each other, and I see that from personal experience," he added. Heathrow is the UK's busiest airport, but its plans to build third runway have faced push-back from climate campaigners and locals despite government support. Woldbye told the Aviation Club UK event that, even though the government backed the expansion, he was "not sure of anything". "We have a very solid description of how that looks and what we're going to build. "What we don't have is necessary modernisation of airspace. "We don't have a long-term regulation which is clear in terms of building a business case," he said. He added that "lots of stuff has to be done this year in order to enable the actual commencement of building". Some 84 million passengers a year fly from Heathrow, but the airport says it could increase this to 150 million with a third runway. The government has said the expansion is badly needed to help economic growth, though some economists dispute this. Others are opposed to the expansion on environmental grounds and on the disruption it would cause to people who currently live near the airport. While the expansion may be a long way off, Woldbye told Aviation Club UK there were still "soft skills" the airport could use to manage more passengers â€” such stopping Europeans and Brits from bumping into each other. "We can change that," he said, adding the airport needs to make sure people going one way keep to a different side to people going the other direction. "And I know that's simplified, but that's the sort of thinking that you need." "We measure crowding, but we also measure people's perceptions of crowding... And interestingly, Terminal 3 has the best score and Terminal 5 has one of the worst, and there's way more space in Terminal 5, so what are we doing wrong?" He said the airport has removed some seating, telephone boxes, and other "clutter" in Terminal 3 and that it feels less crowded as a result, even though there are more people. "People feel more at ease... because they can see where they are going," he added.

    [Original] Amazon's Ring ends deal with surveillance firm after backlash
    Amazonâ€™s Ring has terminated its planned partnership with Flock Safety, a surveillance technology firm, following intense public backlash over privacy concerns. The collaboration, announced in October, would have enabled law enforcement agencies using Flockâ€™s network of cameras and license plate readers to access video from Ring doorbell devices, provided customers had consented. However, Ring stated the integration â€œnever launched,â€ meaning no customer footage was ever shared with Flock. The decision comes days after a Ring Super Bowl ad for its â€œSearch Partyâ€ feature sparked widespread criticism, with critics calling it â€œcreepyâ€ and likening it to dystopian surveillance. The ad depicted neighbors using the tool to locate a lost dog, prompting Senator Ed Markey (D-Mass.) to demand Amazon discontinue its monitoring features, warning of a â€œcreepy surveillance state.â€ The Electronic Frontier Foundation condemned the ad as a cover for a future where biometric identification could be used to track people and pets. Social media users and competitors, including Wyze, mocked the ad, with Wyzeâ€™s satirical videoâ€”viewed nearly 100,000 timesâ€”highlighting the irony of using surveillance tech for trivial purposes. Flock, founded in 2017, operates in over 5,000 U.S. cities and primarily serves law enforcement agencies. The company called the cancellation a â€œmutual decision,â€ stating it allows both firms to better serve their customers. Ring, acquired by Amazon in 2018, has faced ongoing scrutiny over its privacy policies, especially amid heightened immigration enforcement under former President Donald Trump. The company previously reported receiving thousands of law enforcement requests annually for customer footage, complying when legally required. Flock had also come under fire from Senator Ron Wyden (D-Ore.), who accused the company of enabling misuse, including targeting immigrants and women under abortion-related laws. Despite the partnershipâ€™s cancellation, both companies continue to face public skepticism over their roles in surveillance and cooperation with law enforcement.

    [Original] Inflation eases in US as prices for used cars fall
    Inflation in the United States slowed to 2.4% year-on-year in January, down from 2.7% in December and the lowest level since May, according to the Department of Labor. The Consumer Price Index (CPI) report showed cooling prices for energy, which fell 1.5%, and used cars and trucks, which dropped 1.8%. Rents rose 0.2% from December, moderating from a 0.4% increase the prior month. Prices for key grocery staples also eased, with steak prices falling over 2% from December and eggs dropping more than 34% from January 2025. Despite these declines, prices for personal servicesâ€”such as dry cleaning and haircutsâ€”rose 1.6% from December to January and were up nearly 7% year-on-year. Airfares, cigarettes, and music streaming subscriptions also saw notable increases. Analysts noted that while inflation remains above the Federal Reserveâ€™s 2% targetâ€”missed for 59 consecutive monthsâ€”recent data suggests progress. Neil Birrell, chief investment officer at Premier Miton Investors, said the report â€œeases the path towards a cut in rates sooner rather than later,â€ citing strong growth, a resilient job market, and stable inflation. The White House celebrated the data, with President Donald Trump declaring the U.S. economy â€œunbelievableâ€ and claiming he had â€œdefeated Joe Bidenâ€™s inflation crisis.â€ The administration urged the Fed to make â€œlong-overdueâ€ rate cuts, which markets currently expect in June. However, risks remain. Atakan Bakiskan of Berenberg warned that persistent labor shortages could drive wage growth, pushing up services inflation and hindering progress toward the 2% target. He noted that while recent data â€œcould not have started on a better footing,â€ the Fed is not out of the woods. Commodities prices, excluding food and energy, remained stable, suggesting no immediate impact from tariffs. Still, analysts caution that full tariff pass-through to consumers or continued labor market tightness could stall inflationâ€™s decline.

    [Original] Hello Kitty designer steps down after 46 years
    ShareSaveEmer MoreauBusiness reporterShareSaveGetty ImagesHello Kitty is a globally recognised brandThe designer behind Hello Kitty is stepping down after 46 years, during which time she oversaw the feline character achieving world recognition. Yuko Yamaguchi took over design duties for the character - who isn't actually a cat, but a little girl from London - in 1980, five years after she first launched. Hello Kitty was immediately popular in Japan, but over Yamaguchi's four-decade tenure it has become one of the most lucrative franchises in the world. Sanrio, the firm behind Hello Kitty, said Yamaguchi had "passed the baton to the next generation" as it thanked her for her work. Hello Kitty launched as the Japanese 'kawaii' culture - a hyperfeminine, girlish aesthetic - gained popularity across the world. Yamaguchi herself often wore Kitty-style dresses in public and piled her hair in buns. The designer, now aged 70, "has grown Hello Kitty into a character loved by everyone", Sanrio said on its website. The company said one of its other designers, known only by the pseudonym "Aya", had already been working alongside Yamaguchi and will take over by the end of 2026. Yamaguchi will stay with Sanrio to provide advice and support. The Hello Kitty character first appeared on a coin purse in 1980 and has become a global marketing phenomenon. She has appeared on clothes, accessories, video games, and even an Airbus plane. She has partnered with brands such as Unicef, Nintendo and Balenciaga, and has also appeared in float form at the Macy's thanksgiving parade. There are Hello Kitty cafes around the world and a theme park in Japan. Another park is due to open in China. Unlike other Japanese exports such as Pokemon, there is little backstory to the character of Hello Kitty. Sanrio has said she "isn't a human, [but] she's not quite a cat either". She was born in London and has a twin sister named Mimmy and a boyfriend named Dear Daniel, according to Sanrio. Kitty will make her cinematic debut in a Warner Bros film in 2028. She has already appeared in several animated series but has never spoken, as she doesn't have a mouth.

    [Original] Trump revokes key climate ruling which he says has made cars pricier
    President Donald Trump has reversed the 2009 â€œendangerment findingâ€ by the Environmental Protection Agency (EPA), a cornerstone of Obama-era climate policy that declared greenhouse gases like carbon dioxide and methane a threat to public health. The move, labeled by the White House as the â€œlargest deregulation in American history,â€ aims to reduce vehicle costs by $2,400 per unit and save over $1 trillion in economic impact, according to administration claims. Trump called the finding a â€œdisastrous Obama era policyâ€ that harmed the auto industry and fueled the â€œGreen New Scam,â€ a term he used to dismiss Democratic climate initiatives. Former President Barack Obama countered that repealing the finding would make Americans less safe, less healthy, and less able to combat climate change, warning it would benefit the fossil fuel industry. The endangerment finding has served as the legal foundation for federal regulations across sectors including vehicles, power plants, oil and gas, landfills, and aviation. Environmentalists, including the Environmental Defense Fund, argue the rollback will cost Americans $1.4 trillion in additional fuel expenses and lead to up to 58,000 premature deaths and 37 million more asthma attacks. Critics also question the economic logic, noting that shifting manufacturing to countries like China and India may not reduce global emissions. Legal experts, including Meghan Greenfield of Jenner & Block, say the reversal could block future climate lawsuits and state-level climate laws, but anticipate challenges in state courts. The administrationâ€™s justification relied on a Department of Energy panel report criticized for being unrepresentative and scientifically flawed, with a federal judge recently ruling the panelâ€™s formation violated procedural law. Legal analysts believe the administration may welcome litigation, seeking a Supreme Court ruling that would make the reversal permanent, preventing future administrations from reinstating the finding without new legislation. The move marks a sweeping retreat from federal climate regulation, with long-term implications for environmental law and policy.

    [Original] AirAsia accused by artist for allegedly using his work without consent
    Malaysian-based Lithuanian artist Ernest Zacharevic has filed a lawsuit against AirAsia and its parent company, Capital A Berhad, over the unauthorized use of his 2012 street mural, *Kids on Bicycle*, on one of the airlineâ€™s aircraft in late 2024. The artwork, painted in Penangâ€™s George Town heritage district as part of a local festival, depicts two laughing children on a real bicycle, seamlessly integrated into the street scene. The mural has become a tourist attraction, frequently photographed by visitors. Zacharevic alleges that AirAsia reproduced and publicly displayed his work as part of a plane livery without his consent, authorization, or licensing. He first became aware of the use in October 2024 when he saw the aircraft at an airport and later posted images on social media, tagging the airline and demanding a conversation. The livery was removed after he raised public concerns. Zacharevic stated the use felt â€œa little offensiveâ€ and emphasized that the artwork is not a generic or cultural symbol but a unique creation born of years of professional training and labor. He claims this is not the first instance of AirAsia using his work without permission, citing prior use on a food delivery bag for the airlineâ€™s in-flight service. Court documents reveal that Zacharevic had discussions with AirAsia in 2017 about creating art for its jets and offices, during which he provided details of his work and business rates. Despite this, the airline allegedly proceeded to reproduce his principal work in a prominent public setting, infringing on his copyright and moral rights. Zacharevic is seeking compensation through the courts, stressing he does not view the use as a mere cultural reference. AirAsia, Asiaâ€™s largest low-cost carrier with over 200 jets serving more than 100 destinations, has not commented on the lawsuit. The airline recently announced plans to resume flights from Kuala Lumpur to London via Bahrain, marking its return to the UK market after more than a decade.

    [Original] How China Built a Chip Industry, and Why Itâ€™s Still Not Enough
    At a January conference at Tsinghua University, top Chinese AI executives from Tencent, Alibaba, and Zhipu AI expressed optimism about the nationâ€™s AI future, despite a critical bottleneck: access to advanced semiconductors. Chinese chipmakers are projected to produce only a small fraction of the advanced chips made by foreign firms this year, with Huawei estimating it will take nearly two more years to match current Nvidia chip performance. Despite U.S. export controls restricting access to key toolsâ€”especially ASMLâ€™s advanced lithography machines, which remain blocked due to U.S.-led pressureâ€”China continues to pour resources into self-sufficiency. Over $150 billion has been invested since the 2014 launch of a national semiconductor strategy, with the goal of full domestic supply chain control by 2030. Chinese tech giants like Huawei, Alibaba, and ByteDance have launched chip design units, while dozens of factories are being built and top engineers recruited from Taiwan and South Korea. However, production lags significantly: Chinese firms are expected to make just 2% as many AI chips as foreign counterparts and only 1/70th the memory chip capacity, with Samsung, SK Hynix, and TSMC dominating global output. The U.S. has tightened restrictions across three administrations, citing national security concerns, exemplified by the 2017 ZTE fine and the 2019 Huawei blacklisting. In December, President Trump allowed limited Nvidia chip sales to China, but broad access remains uncertain ahead of his planned visit. While Huawei has developed chips comparable to older Nvidia models, they still rely on foreign components. Chinese firms are compensating by linking less powerful chips together and building state-run â€œintelligent computing clusters,â€ but manufacturing volume and quality remain issues, with SMIC struggling to produce defect-free, energy-efficient chips. Startups are attempting to develop domestic equipment, and some researchers report efficiency gains in chip integration. Zhipu AI recently unveiled a model built entirely on Huaweiâ€™s chips, while companies like Zhipu and Minimax are spending more on cloud computingâ€”via Alibaba and Amazonâ€”than they earn in revenue. Despite progress, the AI industryâ€™s growth remains constrained by the semiconductor gap.

    [Original] Bessent Pushes Senate on Fed Confirmation Amid Backlash Over Criminal Inquiry
    Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent urged Senate Republicans on Friday to move forward with confirmation hearings for Kevin M. Warsh, President Trumpâ€™s pick to be the next Federal Reserve chair, and suggested that Jerome H. Powell, the current chair, might not face criminal charges related to his handling of the renovations of the central bankâ€™s headquarters. The comments, made on CNBC, underscored the urgency the Trump administration is feeling as it looks to replace Mr. Powell, whose term expires this year, with Mr. Warsh. Mr. Bessent said that the Senate could open its own investigation into Mr. Powell and suggested that the outcome of the criminal inquiry was not yet clear. Senator Thom Tillis, Republican of North Carolina, is holding up the process of confirming Mr. Warsh because he thinks the investigation into Mr. Powell is not legitimate. â€œThere were subpoenas issued, but that doesnâ€™t have to mean that there are charges,â€ Mr. Bessent said on CNBC. He said he had discussed the idea of a Senate investigation with Republican lawmakers this week. The Treasury secretary added that the White House did not have influence on the fate of the investigation. The inquiry was initiated by the U.S. attorneyâ€™s office in the District of Columbia and approved in November by Jeanine Pirro, a longtime ally of Mr. Trumpâ€™s who was appointed to run the office last year. â€œWeâ€™ll see where the investigation goes with Jeanine Pirroâ€™s office,â€ Mr. Bessent said. He said he believed the Senate Banking Committee would still hold confirmation hearings for Mr. Warsh. However, Mr. Tillis, who is a member of that committee, has the power to prevent the nomination from proceeding to the full Senate for a vote. The criminal inquiry represented a striking incursion on the independence of the Federal Reserve and muddled Mr. Bessentâ€™s hopes of steering a smooth transition process at the central bank. The Treasury secretary led the process of finding a successor for Mr. Powell, who still has the option of remaining on the Fedâ€™s board of governors until 2028, and has been calling for sweeping reforms to the central bank. Mr. Tillis indicated this week that he had no intention of backing down from his pledge to block Mr. Warshâ€™s nomination while Mr. Powell was under investigation. â€œWe have to have an independent Fed,â€ Mr. Tillis said on Thursday. â€œThey either need to move forward with a compelling investigation that convinces me I was wrong, or resolve the current investigation and move on.â€ Alan Rappeport is an economic policy reporter for The Times, based in Washington.     About 800 Jewish settlers live among 33,000 Palestinians in Hebronâ€™s H2 area, a flashpoint in the occupied West Bank. This includes the Cave of the Patriarchs, located within the Ibrahimi Mosque. Al-Sharabati said Palestinian authorities were excluded from decisions, receiving details only from Israeli media. In H2, a tense, divided zone, Palestinian shops are shuttered and streets closed to protect settlers.     [Original] Oil refinery ablaze in Cuba as fuel crisis deepens
    ShareSaveHarry SekulichShareSaveGetty ImagesA fire at an oil refinery in Cuba has been brought under control quickly, as the country faces a deepening fuel crisis. Black smoke clouds could be seen billowing from the Ã‘ico LÃ³pez Refinery in the capital Havana on Friday. No-one was injured and an investigation into the blaze has been set up, Cuba's energy ministry said in a post on X. The fire occurred close to where two oil tankers were moored in Havana Bay. Cuba's fuel shortage has worsened after the US blocked Venezuela's oil shipments to the island. Venezuela, a long-standing ally of Cuba, was previously believed to have sent around 35,000 barrels of oil a day to the island before the US military seized former president NicolÃ¡s Maduro in a raid in the capital, Caracas, on 3 January. The US also confiscated Venezuelan oil tankers, cutting off key supplies of fuel to Cuba, and pledged new tariffs on countries that sell oil to Cuba. US President Donald Trump has urged Cuban leaders to "make a deal" or face consequences, after flows of oil and money from Venezuela were cut off. Electricity cuts have impacted hospital emergency wards, dialysis patients, and pumping stations amid the latest supply shortfalls. Cuba also has a shortage of aviation fuel, leading several airlines to suspend services to the island and some countries, including the UK, to warn citizens against non-essential travel there. "Authorities have introduced fuel rationing, scaled back public services, and made temporary changes to healthcare, education, transport and tourism operations to conserve severely limited energy supplies," the UK's Foreign, Commonwealth and Development Office warned recently. As the oil embargo continues to make life and access to essentials difficult on Cuba, two Mexican ships carrying 800 tonnes of humanitarian aid arrived at Havana Bay on Thursday. United Nations (UN) human rights experts have described Washington's restrictions on oil imports into Cuba as an "extreme form of unilateral economic coercion". UN human rights chief Volker TÃ¼rk said he was "extremely concerned" about the worsening crisis in Cuba. US economic and trade embargoes on Cuba have been in place since 1960.

    [Original] Six possible effects of Trump's climate policy change
    US President Donald Trump has reversed the 2009 EPA endangerment finding, a cornerstone of Obama-era environmental policy that classified six greenhouse gasesâ€”including carbon dioxide and methaneâ€”as threats to public health and the environment. The decision, announced by the Environmental Protection Agency (EPA), removes the legal foundation for regulating emissions under the Clean Air Act, paving the way for looser standards on industries, particularly automakers. The EPA stated that maintaining emissions standards is not essential to its "core mission," despite scientific consensus that greenhouse gases contribute to health issues and premature deaths. The Environmental Defense Fund projects that, without the finding, the US could emit an additional 7.5 to 18 billion tonnes of greenhouse gases by 2055â€”three times current annual emissionsâ€”potentially causing 15,400 to 58,000 premature deaths and tens of millions of additional asthma attacks. The rollback is expected to reduce vehicle manufacturing costs by approximately $2,400 per car, according to the White House, and has been welcomed by industry groups like Ford and the Alliance for Automotive Innovation, which claim current standards are unachievable. However, experts warn the move undermines global competitiveness, as the US retreats from clean vehicle standards while the EU and China expand electric vehicle production and renewable energy deployment. Former EPA head Margo Oge and former Secretary of State John Kerry criticized the reversal, with Kerry stating it will â€œcost livesâ€ and damage global climate efforts. The decision also risks reviving public nuisance lawsuits, which were previously precluded by a 2011 Supreme Court ruling that granted the EPA exclusive authority over emissions regulation. Legal experts, including University of Marylandâ€™s Robert Percival, warn the rollback may backfire. While the administration claims the move will save American taxpayers over $1.3 trillion, critics argue that shifting manufacturing to countries like China and Indiaâ€”where production is dirtierâ€”does not reduce global emissions. Diana Furchtgott-Roth, a former DOT official, echoed this, noting that emissions simply relocate rather than disappear. The policy shift is expected to face legal challenges from environmental groups, and experts stress that abandoning science-based regulation will have long-term economic and health consequences.

    [Original] The war dead pictured on banned Ukrainian athlete's helmet
    Ukrainian skeleton slider Vladyslav Heraskevych has been disqualified from the Winter Olympics in Milan-Cortina after refusing to remove his helmet, which bears the names and images of 22 athletes killed since Russiaâ€™s full-scale invasion of Ukraine. The ban, upheld after his appeal was denied, marks the Gamesâ€™ most significant controversy to date. Heraskevych, who wore the helmet as a â€œhelmet of remembrance,â€ honored athletes including teenage weightlifter Alina Perehudova, boxer Pavlo Ishchenko, and ice hockey player Oleksiy Lohinov, many of whom were his friends. Among those memorialized are Androschuk, a 22-year-old decathlete who died in Bakhmut in January 2023; Bakhur, a 17-year-old kickboxing champion killed in Kharkiv; and 11-year-old gymnast Diachenko, who died in Mariupol in March 2022 during a Russian air strike. Halinichev, a 2018 Youth Olympic silver medalist and 2021 European U22 champion, died in Luhansk in 2023 after volunteering for the army despite injuries. Ishchenko, a four-time strongman champion and coach, was killed in 2023. Nine-year-old judo enthusiast Ivashko died in Kyiv in June 2023; 31-year-old shooter Khabarov, a national record holder, died in Donetsk in 2023. Kononenko, a strongman and actor, died in February 2024 after returning to combat following a severe injury. Diver Kozubenko, 34, and dancer Kurdel, 20, were killed in Kryvyi Rih and Kharkiv, respectively. Track cyclist Kutsenko, 34, died in 2024 after returning to Ukraine from Italy. Fifteen-year-old ballroom dancer Lebid was killed in Dnipro in 2023. Lohinov, 23, former ice hockey goalkeeper, died in Luhansk. Malyshev, 19, a former biathlete, died in Kharkiv and was posthumously awarded the Order For Courage. Perehudova, 14, was preparing for a European Championship when she died in Mariupol. Polischchuk, a high jumper, died in March 2023. Sharpar, 25, a figure skater, died near Bakhmut in 2023. Shpuk, 34, a veteran sports coach and Invictus Games participant, died in November 2023. Troyan, 32, a former athlete and IT professional, died in June 2025 after operating drones on over 1,000 combat missions. Greco-Roman wrestler Yaremenko, 25, died days before his birthday and aspired to become a coach. Fencer Yepifanov, 18, died in 2023. Thirteen-year-old Zui, a boxer and football player, died in Mariupol in March 2022. The memorial, compiled by Angels of Sport, underscores the profound human cost of the war on Ukraineâ€™s athletic community.

    [Original] Why the BNP won Bangladeshâ€™s post-uprising election
    Bangladeshâ€™s 13th parliamentary election, held in February 2026, delivered a decisive victory for the Bangladesh Nationalist Party (BNP), ending 15 years of rule by Sheikh Hasinaâ€™s Awami League. The BNP secured a plurality, returning to power after years in opposition, though not through a sweeping national revolution. The outcome was shaped less by ideological transformation than by voter frustration, strategic candidate selection, and the mechanics of the first-past-the-post (FPTP) system. While the mass uprising that toppled Hasina in August 2024 created political momentum, it did not result in a permanent realignment of voter loyalties. Instead, the election reflected a hybrid of normal electoral patterns with significant deviations. The Bangladesh Jamaat-e-Islami (JI) achieved a historic performance, winning 68 seats and securing 77 seats in total through its coalition, a dramatic improvement from its previous high of 18 seats in 1991. This surge was fueled by widespread anger over BNPâ€™s local misconductâ€”allegations of corruption, extortion, and arroganceâ€”particularly in rural areas, prompting defections from BNP loyalists and swing voters. However, Jamaatâ€™s regional concentration and strategic missteps limited its national reach. Its inconsistent stance on womenâ€™s rights and revisionist rhetoric on the 1971 Liberation War alienated broad swaths of the electorate, including conservative families, undermining its potential for a national wave. Despite its gains, Jamaat could not overcome FPTPâ€™s structural barriers. BNPâ€™s victory was rooted in its organizational depth and strategic nomination of established leaders with strong grassroots networks. Rural voters, pragmatic and embedded in patronage systems, favored familiar figures over ideologically pure but unknown candidates. Urban, educated voters were drawn to Jamaatâ€™s moral branding, but their influence was outweighed by rural pragmatism. The Awami League, though weakened, retained a residual vote of 5â€“7% hardliners and an additional 20â€“25% undecided or reluctant to disclose preferences. Many non-hardcore AL voters defected to BNP not out of ideology but instrumental rationalityâ€”seeking access to services through the likely winning party. This perception of BNPâ€™s inevitability became self-fulfilling. The National Citizen Party (NCP), emerging from the 2024 uprising, won five seatsâ€”a notable achievement for a new party in a polarized environment. Under proportional representation, it might have fared better, but FPTP capped its potential. Ultimately, BNPâ€™s win was not a mandate for renewal but a reflection of the limits of anger, moral branding, and revisionismâ€”and the enduring power of organizational strength in a winner-take-all system.
